10029{_}985{_}"The door locked on the inside," Morriston suggested.
10029{_}986{_}"That, I grant you, is at first sight mysterious enough," Henshaw returned, his keen eyes fixed on Morriston.
10029{_}987{_}"But even that does not reconcile me to the monstrous improbability of my brother, Clement, taking his own life.
10029{_}988{_}I knew him too well to admit that."
10029{_}989{_}"Unfortunately," Morriston replied, sympathetically restraining any approach to an argumentative tone, "your brother was practically a stranger to me, and to us all.
10029{_}990{_}My friends here, Captain Kelson and Mr. Gifford, met him casually at the railway station and drove with him to the _Golden Lion_ in the town, where they all put up."
10029{_}991{_}Henshaw's sharp scrutiny was immediately transferred from Morriston to his companions.
10029{_}992{_}"Can you, gentlemen, throw any light on the matter?"
10029{_}993{_}he asked sharply.
10029{_}994{_}"None at all, I am sorry to say," Kelson answered readily.
10029{_}995{_}"I may as well tell you how our very slight acquaintance with him came about."
10029{_}996{_}"If you please," Henshaw responded, in a tone more of command than request.
10029{_}997{_}Kelson, naturally ignoring his questioner's slightly offensive manner, thereupon related the circumstances of the encounter at the station-yard and of the subsequent drive to the town, merely softening the detail of their preliminary altercation.
10029{_}998{_}Henshaw listened alertly intent, it seemed, to seize upon any point which did not satisfy him.
10029{_}999{_}"That was all you saw of my unfortunate brother?"
10029{_}1000{_}he demanded at the end.
10029{_}1001{_}"We saw him for a few moments in the hall of the hotel just as we were starting," Kelson answered.
10029{_}1002{_}"You drove here together?
10029{_}1003{_}No?"
10029{_}1004{_}"No; your brother took an hotel carriage, and I drove in my own trap."
10029{_}1005{_}"With Mr.
10029{_}1006{_}----?"
10029{_}1007{_}he indicated Gifford, who up to this point had not spoken.
10029{_}1008{_}"No," Gifford answered.
10029{_}1009{_}"I came on later.
10029{_}1010{_}A suit-case with my evening things had gone astray--been carried on in the train, and I had to wait till it was returned."
10029{_}1011{_}Henshaw stared at him for a moment sharply as though the statement had about it something vaguely suspicious, seemed about to put another question, checked himself, and turned about with a gesture of perplexity.
10029{_}1012{_}"I don't understand it at all," he muttered.
10029{_}1013{_}Then suddenly facing round again he said sharply to Gifford, "Have you anything to add, sir, to what your friend has told me?"
10029{_}1014{_}"I can say nothing more," Gifford answered.
10029{_}1015{_}Henshaw turned away again, and seemed as though but half satisfied.
10029{_}1016{_}"The facts," he said in a lawyer-like tone, "don't appear to lead us far.
10029{_}1017{_}But when ascertained facts stop short they may be supplemented.
10029{_}1018{_}Apart from what is actually known--I ask this as the dead man's only brother--have either of you gentlemen formed any idea as to how he came by his death?"
10029{_}1019{_}He was looking at Morriston, his cross-examining manner now softened by the human touch.
10029{_}1020{_}"It has not occurred to me to look beyond what seems the obvious explanation of suicide," Morriston answered frankly.
10029{_}1021{_}Henshaw turned to Kelson.
10029{_}1022{_}"And you, sir; have you any idea beyond the known facts?"
10029{_}1023{_}"None," was the answer, "except that he took his own life.
10029{_}1024{_}The door locked on--"  Henshaw interrupted him sharply.
10029{_}1025{_}"Now you are getting back to the facts, Captain Kelson.
10029{_}1026{_}I tell you the idea of my brother Clement taking his own life is to me absolutely inconceivable.
10029{_}1027{_}Have you any idea, however far-fetched, as to what really may have happened?"
10029{_}1028{_}Kelson shook his head.
10029{_}1029{_}"None.
10029{_}1030{_}Except I must say he looked to me the last man who would do such an act."
10029{_}1031{_}"I should think so," Henshaw returned decidedly.
10029{_}1032{_}Then he addressed himself to Gifford.
10029{_}1033{_}"I must ask you, sir, the same question."
10029{_}1034{_}"And I can give you no more satisfactory answer," Gifford said.
10029{_}1035{_}"As a man with knowledge of the world as I take you to be?"
10029{_}1036{_}Henshaw urged keenly.
10029{_}1037{_}"No."
10029{_}1038{_}"At least you agree with your friend here, that my poor brother did not strike one as being a man liable to make away with himself?"
10029{_}1039{_}"Certainly.
10029{_}1040{_}But one can never tell.
10029{_}1041{_}I knew nothing of him or his affairs."
10029{_}1042{_}"But I did," Henshaw retorted vehemently.
10029{_}1043{_}"And I tell you, gentlemen, the thing is utterly impossible.
10029{_}1044{_}But we shall see.
10029{_}1045{_}The body--is it here?"
10029{_}1046{_}"The police have charge of it in the room where he was found.
10029{_}1047{_}It is to be removed at nightfall.
10029{_}1048{_}You will wish to see it?"
10029{_}1049{_}Morriston answered.
10029{_}1050{_}"Yes."
10029{_}1051{_}Morriston led the way to the tower, explaining as he went the arrangements on the night of the ball.
10029{_}1052{_}Henshaw spoke little, his mood seemed dissatisfied and resentful, but his sharp eyes seemed to take everything in.
10029{_}1053{_}Once he asked, "Did my brother dance much?"
10029{_}1054{_}"He was introduced to a partner," Morriston replied.
10029{_}1055{_}"But after that no one seems to have noticed him in the ball-room."
10029{_}1056{_}"You mean he disappeared quite early in the evening?"
10029{_}1057{_}"Yes; so far as we have been able to ascertain," Morriston answered.
10029{_}1058{_}"Naturally, before this awful discovery we had been much exercised by his mysterious disappearance and failure to return to the hotel."
10029{_}1059{_}"All the same," Henshaw returned sourly, "one can hardly accept the inference that he came down here for the express purpose of making away with himself in your house."
10029{_}1060{_}"No, I cannot understand it," Morriston replied, as he turned and began to ascend the winding stairway.
10029{_}1061{_}On the threshold of the topmost floor he paused.
10029{_}1062{_}"This is the door we found locked on the inside," he observed quietly.
10029{_}1063{_}Henshaw gave a keen look round, and nodded.
10029{_}1064{_}Morriston pushed open the door and they entered.
10029{_}1065{_}The body of Clement Henshaw still lay on the floor in charge of the detective and the inspector, the third man having been despatched to the town to make arrangements for its removal.
10029{_}1066{_}With a nod to the officials, Henshaw advanced to the body and bent over it.
10029{_}1067{_}"Poor Clement!"
10029{_}1068{_}he murmured.
10029{_}1069{_}After a few moments' scrutiny, Henshaw turned to the officers.
10029{_}1070{_}"I am the brother of the deceased," he said, addressing more particularly the detective.
10029{_}1071{_}"What do you make of this?"
10029{_}1072{_}The question was put in the same sharp, business-like tone which had characterized his utterances in the library.
10029{_}1073{_}"Judging by the door being locked on the inside," the detective answered sympathetically, "it can only be a case of suicide."
10029{_}1074{_}Henshaw frowned.
10029{_}1075{_}"It will take a good deal to persuade me of that," he retorted.
10029{_}1076{_}"Mr. ----"  "Detective-Sergeant Finch."
10029{_}1077{_}"Mr. Finch.
10029{_}1078{_}Did the doctor say suicide?"
10029{_}1079{_}"I did not hear him express a definite opinion.
10029{_}1080{_}Did you, inspector?"
10029{_}1081{_}"No, Mr. Finch.
10029{_}1082{_}I rather presumed the doctor took it for granted."
10029{_}1083{_}"Took it for granted!"
10029{_}1084{_}Henshaw echoed contemptuously.
10029{_}1085{_}"I'm not going to take it for granted, I can tell you.
10029{_}1086{_}Did the doctor examine the body?"
10029{_}1087{_}"He made a cursory examination.
10029{_}1088{_}He is arranging to meet the police surgeon for an autopsy to-morrow morning."
10029{_}1089{_}On the table lay a narrow-bladed chisel, the lower portion of the bright steel discoloured with the dark stain of blood.
10029{_}1090{_}The inspector pointed to it.
10029{_}1091{_}"That is the instrument with which the wound must have been made," he remarked in a subdued tone.
10029{_}1092{_}"It was found lying beside the body."
10029{_}1093{_}Henshaw took it up and ran his eyes over it.
10029{_}1094{_}"How could he have got this?"
10029{_}1095{_}he demanded, looking round with what seemed a distrustful glance.
10029{_}1096{_}"I can only suggest," Morriston answered, "that one of my men must have left it when some work was done here a few days ago."
10029{_}1097{_}"That is so apparently, Mr. Morriston," the detective corroborated.
10029{_}1098{_}"It has been identified by Haynes, the estate carpenter."
10029{_}1099{_}Henshaw put down the chisel and for some moments kept silence, tightening his thin lips as though in strenuous thought.
10029{_}1100{_}Then suddenly he demanded, "Beyond the fact that the door was found locked from within, what reason have you for your conclusion?"
10029{_}1101{_}Mr. Finch shrugged.
10029{_}1102{_}"We don't see how it could be otherwise, sir," he replied with quiet conviction.
10029{_}1103{_}"Clearly the deceased gentleman must have been alone in the room when he died."
10029{_}1104{_}"Might he not have locked the door after the wound was given?"
10029{_}1105{_}Henshaw suggested in a tone of cross-examination.
10029{_}1106{_}"Dr.
10029{_}1107{_}Page was of opinion that death, or at any rate unconsciousness, must have been almost instantaneous," Finch rejoined respectfully.
10029{_}1108{_}"Even supposing the autopsy bears out that view I shall not be satisfied," Henshaw declared.
10029{_}1109{_}The inspector took up the argument.
10029{_}1110{_}"You see, sir, taking into consideration the position of the room it would be impossible for any second party who may have been here with the deceased to leave it undiscovered except by the door.
10029{_}1111{_}To drop from this window, which is the only one large enough to admit of an adult body passing through, would mean pretty certain death.
10029{_}1112{_}Anyhow the party would have been so injured that getting clear away would be out of the question.
10029{_}1113{_}Will you see for yourself, sir?"
10029{_}1114{_}He threw back the window and invited Henshaw to look down.
10029{_}1115{_}The argument seemed conclusive.
10029{_}1116{_}"Was the window found open or shut?"
10029{_}1117{_}"It was found unlatched, sir," Finch answered.
10029{_}1118{_}"But the servants think that it was opened that morning and owing to the extra work in the house that day its fastening in the evening was overlooked."
10029{_}1119{_}"Even if a second person had let himself down from the window," the inspector argued, "the rope would have been here."
10029{_}1120{_}Henshaw kept silence, seemingly indifferent to the officials' arguments.
10029{_}1121{_}"I can only tell you I am far from satisfied with the suicide theory," he said at length.
10029{_}1122{_}"My brother was not that sort of man.
10029{_}1123{_}He had nerves of iron; he was in love with life and all it meant to him, and he made it a rule never to let anything worry him.
10029{_}1124{_}Let the other fellow worry, was his motto.
10029{_}1125{_}Well, we shall see."
10029{_}1126{_}He turned towards the door, and as he did so he caught sight of a cardboard box in which was a collection of various articles, jewellery, a watch and chain, money, a pocket-handkerchief, a letter, and a dance programme.
10029{_}1127{_}"The contents of deceased's pockets," the inspector observed, answering Henshaw's glance of curiosity.
10029{_}1128{_}"We have collected and made a list of them, and they will in due course be handed to you, or to his heir, on the coroner's order."
10029{_}1129{_}"Is that a letter?
10029{_}1130{_}May I see it?"
10029{_}1131{_}As the official hesitated, Henshaw had snatched the paper, a folded note, and rapidly ran his eye through its contents.
10029{_}1132{_}Then he gave a curious laugh, as he turned over the paper as though seeking an address, and laid it back in the box.
10029{_}1133{_}"A note from my brother to an anonymous lady," he observed quietly.
10029{_}1134{_}"Perhaps if we could find out whom it was meant for she would throw some light on the mystery."
10029{_}1135{_}CHAPTER VIII  KELSON'S PERPLEXITY   "What do you think of Mr. Gervase Henshaw?"
10029{_}1136{_}Kelson said, as, late in the afternoon, he and Gifford walked towards the town together.
10029{_}1137{_}Henshaw had left Wynford Place half an hour previously, having kept to the end his attitude of resentful incredulity.
10029{_}1138{_}"A nailer," Gifford answered shortly.
10029{_}1139{_}"Yes," Kelson agreed.
10029{_}1140{_}"He gives one the idea of a man who will make trouble if he can.
10029{_}1141{_}As offensive as his brother was, I should say, although in a different line.
10029{_}1142{_}I did not detect one sign of any consideration for the Morristons in their horribly unpleasant position."
10029{_}1143{_}"No," Gifford agreed.
10029{_}1144{_}"I was very sorry for Morriston.
10029{_}1145{_}He behaved extremely well, considering the irritatingly antagonistic line the man chose to take up."
10029{_}1146{_}"Brainy man, Henshaw; unpleasantly sharp, eh?"
10029{_}1147{_}"Yes," Gifford replied.
10029{_}1148{_}"Added to his legal training he is by way of being an expert in criminology."
10029{_}1149{_}"I do hope," Kelson remarked thoughtfully, "he is not going to make himself unpleasant down here.
10029{_}1150{_}The scandal will be quite enough without that.
10029{_}1151{_}Horribly rough luck on the Morristons as new-comers here to have an affair like this happening in their house.
10029{_}1152{_}I can't think what brought the man down here."
10029{_}1153{_}"No; he came with a purpose, that's certain."
10029{_}1154{_}"A woman in it, no doubt.
10029{_}1155{_}One can quite sympathize with the brother's incredulity as to the suicide theory, though hardly with his manner of showing it.
10029{_}1156{_}The dead man was not that sort.
10029{_}1157{_}The idea is simply staggering."
10029{_}1158{_}Gifford made no response, and for a while they walked on in silence.
10029{_}1159{_}Presently he asked, "How did you get on to-day--I mean with Colonel Tredworth?"
10029{_}1160{_}"Oh, everything went off beautifully," Kelson answered, his tone brightening with the change of subject.
10029{_}1161{_}"The old boy gave me his consent and his blessing.
10029{_}1162{_}I've scarcely been able as yet to appreciate my luck, with this affair at Wynford Place intervening."
10029{_}1163{_}"No," Gifford responded mechanically.
10029{_}1164{_}"It is calculated to drive everything else out of one's head."
10029{_}1165{_}"It is suggested," said Kelson, "that we should be married quite soon.
10029{_}1166{_}The Tredworths are going abroad next month and don't propose to hurry back.
10029{_}1167{_}So it means that if the wedding does not take place before they leave it must be postponed till probably the autumn."
10029{_}1168{_}"I should think the latter would be the best plan."
10029{_}1169{_}Kelson turned quickly to his companion.
10029{_}1170{_}"To postpone it?"
10029{_}1171{_}he exclaimed in a rather hurt tone.
10029{_}1172{_}"Why on earth should we?
10029{_}1173{_}We have nothing to wait for, I mean money or anything of that sort."
10029{_}1174{_}"No; but settlements take a long time to draw up."
10029{_}1175{_}"Not if the lawyers are told to hurry up with them."
10029{_}1176{_}"Then you will have to find a house, and get furniture.
10029{_}1177{_}And there is the trousseau," Gifford urged.
10029{_}1178{_}"Oh," Kelson returned with a show of impatience, "all these details can be got over in two or three weeks if we set ourselves to do it.
10029{_}1179{_}I don't believe in waiting once the thing is settled."
10029{_}1180{_}"I don't believe in rushing matters," Gifford rejoined.
10029{_}1181{_}"Least of all matrimony."
10029{_}1182{_}Kelson stopped dead.
10029{_}1183{_}"Why, Hugh," he said in an expostulatory tone, "what is the matter with you?
10029{_}1184{_}You are most confoundedly unsympathetic.
10029{_}1185{_}Any one would think you did not want me to marry the girl."
10029{_}1186{_}"I certainly don't want you to be in too great a hurry," Gifford returned calmly.

10029{_}1189{_}"I feel it is a mistake."

10029{_}1192{_}"Hardly.

10029{_}1306{_}"The blood stain on my cuff that night.

10029{_}1314{_}CHAPTER IX  THE CLOAK OF NIGHT   At the inquest which was held next day nothing was elicited which could offer any solution of the mystery of Clement Henshaw's death.
10029{_}1315{_}It seemed to be pretty generally accepted to be a case of suicide, although that view was opposed in evidence, not only by Gervase Henshaw on general grounds, but also by the medical witnesses, who had grave doubts whether the mortal wound had been self-inflicted.
10029{_}1316{_}"Just possible but decidedly improbable, both from the position of the wound and the direction of the blow," was Dr.
10029{_}1317{_}Page's opinion.
10029{_}1318{_}It was a downward, oblique stab in the throat which had pierced the larynx and penetrated the jugular vein.
10029{_}1319{_}The deceased would have been unable to cry out and would probably have quickly become insensible from asphyxiation.
10029{_}1320{_}Unless he was left-handed the stab could scarcely have been self-given.
10029{_}1321{_}The police authorities committed themselves to no definite theory at that stage, and at their request the inquiry was adjourned for a month.
10029{_}1322{_}Morriston, leaving the hall with Kelson and Gifford, asked them to walk back with him to Wynford Place.
10029{_}1323{_}"Let us throw off this depressing business as well as we can," he said.
10029{_}1324{_}"Of course I have had to break it to my sister and the others; they would have seen it to-day in print.
10029{_}1325{_}Thank goodness the papers don't look beyond the suicide idea, so they are not making much fuss about it.
10029{_}1326{_}If they took a more sensational view, as I fear they will now after the medical evidence, it would be a terrible nuisance."
10029{_}1327{_}"I hope the ladies were not much upset when you told them," Gifford remarked.
10029{_}1328{_}"Well, they already had an idea that something was seriously wrong, and that took the edge off the announcement.
10029{_}1329{_}Of course they were horribly shocked at the idea of the tragedy so close at hand, though I softened the details as well as I could."
10029{_}1330{_}"If the suicide idea is to be abandoned," said Kelson, speaking with an unusually gloomy, preoccupied air, "the police have an uncommonly difficult and delicate task before them."
10029{_}1331{_}"Yes, indeed," Morriston responded.
10029{_}1332{_}"And I should say that abnormally keen person, the brother, will keep them up to collar."
10029{_}1333{_}"He means to," Kelson replied rather grimly.
10029{_}1334{_}"We had him for an hour last night cross-examining us, naturally to no purpose; we could tell him nothing."
10029{_}1335{_}"He won't leave a stone unturned," Morriston said.
10029{_}1336{_}"He proposes to return here after the funeral in town."
10029{_}1337{_}"And I should say," observed Kelson, "if the mystery is to be solved he is the man to solve it.
10029{_}1338{_}What do you think, Hugh?"
10029{_}1339{_}Gifford seemed to rouse himself by an effort from an absorbing train of thought.
10029{_}1340{_}"Oh, yes," he answered.
10029{_}1341{_}"Except that it is possible to be a little too clever and so overlook the obvious."

10029{_}1343{_}Where is Mr. Gervase Henshaw, or any one else, going to look for the criminal?"
10029{_}1344{_}"Not among your guests, let's hope," Kelson said with a touch of uneasiness.

10029{_}1346{_}I can't tell who may have got a ticket and been present.
10029{_}1347{_}There was a great crowd.

10029{_}1350{_}"Not a pleasant idea," continued Morriston.
10029{_}1351{_}"But I don't see, if a bad character did get in and mix with the company, why he should have done a fellow guest to death, nor how he contrived to leave his victim and get out of the room after he had locked the door."
10029{_}1352{_}"If the two men had a row over a girl, or anything else," Kelson said, "there is still that difficulty to be surmounted."
10029{_}1353{_}Gifford spoke.
10029{_}1354{_}"From what one could judge of the dead man's personality and character it is not a far-fetched supposition that he must have had enemies."
10029{_}1355{_}"Down here?"

10029{_}1438{_}CHAPTER X  AN ALARMING DISCOVERY   Next evening the two friends at the _Golden Lion_ were engaged to dine with the Morristons.
10029{_}1439{_}They had been out with the hounds all day, and, beyond the natural gossip of the country-side, had heard nothing fresh concerning the tragedy.
10029{_}1440{_}Gervase Henshaw had gone up to town for his brother's funeral, and Host Dipper had no fresh development to report.
10029{_}1441{_}In answer to a question from Gifford, he said he expected Mr. Henshaw back on the morrow, or at latest the day after.
10029{_}1442{_}"It is altogether a most mysterious affair," he observed sagely, being free, now that his late guest's perplexing disappearance was accounted for, even in that tragic fashion, to regard the business and to moralize over it without much personal feeling in the matter.
10029{_}1443{_}"I fancy Mr. Gervase Henshaw means to work the police up to getting to the bottom of it.
10029{_}1444{_}For I don't fancy that he is by any means satisfied that his unfortunate brother took his own life.
10029{_}1445{_}And I must say," he added in a pronouncement evidently the fruit of careful deliberation, "I don't know how it strikes you, gentlemen, but from what I saw of the deceased it is hard to imagine him as making away with himself."
10029{_}1446{_}"Yes," Gifford replied.
10029{_}1447{_}"But before any other conclusion can be fairly arrived at the police will have to account for the locked door."
10029{_}1448{_}Evidently Mr. Dipper's lucubrations had not, so far, reached a satisfactory explanation of that puzzle; he could only wag his head and respond generally, "Ah, yes.
10029{_}1449{_}That will be a hard nut for them to crack, I'm thinking."
10029{_}1450{_}The dinner at Wynford Place was made as cheerful as, with the gloom of a tragedy over the house, could be possible.
10029{_}1451{_}"We had the police with a couple of detectives here all this morning," Morriston said, "and a great upset it has been.
10029{_}1452{_}After having made the most minute scrutiny of the room in the tower they had every one of the servants in one by one and put them through a most searching examination.
10029{_}1453{_}But, I imagine, without result.
10029{_}1454{_}No one in the house, and I have questioned most of them casually myself, seems to be able to throw the smallest light on the affair."
10029{_}1455{_}"Have the police arrived at any theory?"
10029{_}1456{_}Gifford inquired.
10029{_}1457{_}"Apparently they have come to no definite conclusion," Morriston answered.
10029{_}1458{_}"They seemed to have an idea, though--to account for the problem of the locked door--that thieves might have got into the house with the object of making a haul in the bedrooms while every one's attention was engaged down below, have secreted themselves in the tower, been surprised by Henshaw, and, to save themselves, have taken the only effectual means of silencing him, poor fellow."
10029{_}1459{_}"Then how, with the door locked on the inside did they make their escape?"
10029{_}1460{_}Miss Morriston asked.
10029{_}1461{_}"That can so far be only a matter of conjecture," her brother answered, with a shrug.
10029{_}1462{_}"Of course they might have provided themselves with some sort of ladder, but there are no signs of it.
10029{_}1463{_}And the height of the window in that top room is decidedly against the theory."
10029{_}1464{_}"We hear at the _Lion_" Kelson remarked, "that the brother, Gervase Henshaw, is returning to-morrow or next day."
10029{_}1465{_}Morriston did not receive the news with any appearance of satisfaction.
10029{_}1466{_}"I hope he won't come fussing about here," he said, with a touch of protest.
10029{_}1467{_}"Making every allowance for the sudden shock under which he was labouring I thought his attitude the other day most objectionable, didn't you?"
10029{_}1468{_}"I did most certainly," Gifford answered promptly.
10029{_}1469{_}"His manners struck me as deplorable," Kelson agreed.
10029{_}1470{_}"Yes," their host continued.
10029{_}1471{_}"It never seemed to occur to the fellow that some little sympathy was due also to us.
10029{_}1472{_}But he seemed rather to suggest that the tragedy was our fault.
10029{_}1473{_}In ordinary circumstances I should have dealt pretty shortly with him.
10029{_}1474{_}But it was not worth while."
10029{_}1475{_}"No," Kelson observed, "All the same, you need not allow a continuation of his behaviour."
10029{_}1476{_}"I don't intend to," Morriston replied with decision.
10029{_}1477{_}"I hope the man won't want to come ferreting in the place; that may well be left to the police; but if he does I can't very well refuse him leave.
10029{_}1478{_}He must be free of the house, or at any rate of the tower."
10029{_}1479{_}"Or," put in Kelson, "he'll have a grievance against you, and accuse you of trying to burk the mystery."
10029{_}1480{_}"Is he a very objectionable person?"
10029{_}1481{_}Miss Morriston asked.
10029{_}1482{_}"We passed one another in the hall as he left the house and I received what seemed a rather unmannerly stare."
10029{_}1483{_}Her brother laughed.
10029{_}1484{_}"My dear Edith, the type of man you would simply loathe.
10029{_}1485{_}Abnormally, unpleasantly sharp and suspicious; with a cleverness which takes no account of tact or politeness, he questions you as though you were in the witness-box and he a criminal barrister trying to trap you.
10029{_}1486{_}I don't know whether he behaves more civilly to ladies, but from our experience of the man I should recommend you to keep out of his way."
10029{_}1487{_}"I shall," his sister replied.
10029{_}1488{_}"I should say no respecter of persons--or anything else," Kelson remarked with a laugh.
10029{_}1489{_}"Let us hope he won't take it into his head to worry us," Miss Morriston said with quiet indifference.
10029{_}1490{_}"I am sorry to see," Morriston observed later on when the ladies had left them, "that the papers are beginning to take a sensational view of the affair."
10029{_}1491{_}"Yes," Kelson responded; "we noticed that.
10029{_}1492{_}It will be a nuisance for you."
10029{_}1493{_}"The trouble has already begun," his host continued somewhat ruefully.
10029{_}1494{_}"We have had two or three reporters here to-day worrying the servants with all sorts of absurd questions.
10029{_}1495{_}It is, of course, all to be accounted for by the medical evidence.
10029{_}1496{_}That has put them on the scent of what they will no doubt call a sensational development.
10029{_}1497{_}So long as it looked like nothing beyond suicide there was not so much likelihood of public interest in the case."
10029{_}1498{_}"The police--" Gifford began.
10029{_}1499{_}"The police," Morriston took up the word, "are fairly nonplussed.
10029{_}1500{_}It seems the farther they get the less obvious does the suicide theory become.
10029{_}1501{_}Well, we shall see."
10029{_}1502{_}"In the meantime I'm afraid you and Miss Morriston are in for a heap of undeserved annoyance," Kelson observed sympathetically.
10029{_}1503{_}"Yes," Morriston agreed gloomily; "I am sorry for Edith; she is plucky, and feels it, I expect, far more than she cares to show."
10029{_}1504{_}When the men went into the drawing-room Muriel Tredworth made a sign to Kelson; he joined her and, sitting down some distance apart from the rest, they carried on in low tones what seemed to be a serious conversation.
10029{_}1505{_}"I want to tell you of something extraordinary which has happened to me, Hugh."
10029{_}1506{_}Gifford just caught the words as the girl led the way out of earshot.
10029{_}1507{_}He had noticed that she had been rather preoccupied during dinner, an unusual mood for so lively a girl, and now he could not help watching the pair in the distance, she talking with an earnest, troubled expression, and he listening to her story in grave wonderment, now and again interposing a few words.
10029{_}1508{_}Once they looked at Gifford, and he was certain they were speaking of him.
10029{_}1509{_}With the gloom of a tragedy over the house the little party could not be very festive; avoid it as they set themselves to do, the brooding subject could not be ignored, general conversation flagged, and it soon became time for the visitors to say good-night.
10029{_}1510{_}As they walked back to the town together Gifford noticed that his companion was unusually silent, and he tactfully forbore to break in upon his preoccupation.
10029{_}1511{_}At length Kelson spoke.
10029{_}1512{_}"Muriel has just been telling me of an unpleasant and unaccountable thing which happened to her this evening.
10029{_}1513{_}A discovery of a rather alarming character.
10029{_}1514{_}I said I would take your advice about it, Hugh, and she agreed."
10029{_}1515{_}"Does it concern the affair at Wynford?"
10029{_}1516{_}"It may," Kelson answered in a perplexed tone; "and yet I don't well see how it can.
10029{_}1517{_}Anyhow it is uncommonly mysterious.
10029{_}1518{_}We won't talk about it here," he added gravely, "but wait till we get in."
10029{_}1519{_}"Miss Morriston looked well to-night," Gifford remarked, falling in with his friend's wish to postpone the more engrossing subject.
10029{_}1520{_}"Yes," Kelson agreed casually.
10029{_}1521{_}"She takes this ghastly business quietly enough.
10029{_}1522{_}But that is her way."
10029{_}1523{_}"I have been wondering," Gifford said, "how much she cares for Painswick.
10029{_}1524{_}He is manifestly quite smitten, but I doubt her being nearly as keen on him."
10029{_}1525{_}Kelson laughed.
10029{_}1526{_}"If you ask me I don't think she cares a bit for him.
10029{_}1527{_}And one can scarcely be surprised.
10029{_}1528{_}He is not a bad fellow, but rather a prig, and Edith Morriston is not exactly the sort of girl to suffer that type of man gladly.
10029{_}1529{_}But her brother is all for the match; from Painswick's point of view she is just the wife for him, money and a statuesque style of beauty; altogether I shall be surprised if it does not come off."
10029{_}1530{_}"They are not engaged, then?"
10029{_}1531{_}"I think not.
10029{_}1532{_}They say he proposes regularly once a week.
10029{_}1533{_}But she holds him off."
10029{_}1534{_}Arrived at the _Golden Lion_ they went straight up to Kelson's room, where with more curiosity than he quite cared to show, Gifford settled himself to hear what the other had to tell him.
10029{_}1535{_}"I dare say you noticed how worried Muriel looked all dinner-time," Kelson began.
10029{_}1536{_}"I thought that what had happened in the house had got on her nerves; but it was something worse than that; I mean touching her more nearly."
10029{_}1537{_}"Tell me," Gifford said quietly.
10029{_}1538{_}"You know," Kelson proceeded, "they are going to this dance at Hasborough to-morrow.
10029{_}1539{_}Well, it appears that when her maid was overhauling her ball-dress, the same she wore here the other night, she found blood stains on it."
10029{_}1540{_}"That," Gifford remarked coolly, "may satisfactorily account for the marks on your cuff."
10029{_}1541{_}Kelson stared in surprise at the other's coolness.
10029{_}1542{_}"I dare say it does," he exclaimed with a touch of impatience.
10029{_}1543{_}"I had hardly connected the two.
10029{_}1544{_}But what do you think of this?
10029{_}1545{_}How in the name of all that's mysterious can it be accounted for?"
10029{_}1546{_}"Hardly by the idea that Miss Tredworth had anything to do with the late tragedy," was the quiet answer.
10029{_}1547{_}"Good heavens, man, I should hope not," Kelson cried vehemently.
10029{_}1548{_}"That is too monstrously absurd."
10029{_}1549{_}"What is Miss Tredworth's idea?"
10029{_}1550{_}"She has none.
10029{_}1551{_}She is completely mystified.
10029{_}1552{_}And inclined to be horribly frightened."
10029{_}1553{_}"Naturally," Gifford commented in the same even tone.
10029{_}1554{_}His manner seemed to irritate Kelson.
10029{_}1555{_}"I wish, my dear Hugh, I could take it half as coolly as you do," he exclaimed resentfully.
10029{_}1556{_}"I don't know what you want me to do or say, Harry," Gifford expostulated.
10029{_}1557{_}"The whole affair is so utterly mysterious that I can't pretend even to hazard an explanation."
10029{_}1558{_}"In the meantime Muriel and I are in the most appalling position.
10029{_}1559{_}Why, man, she may at any moment be arrested on suspicion if this discovery leaks out, as it is sure to do."
10029{_}1560{_}"You can't try to hush it up; that would be a fatal mistake," Gifford said thoughtfully, "and would immediately arouse suspicion."
10029{_}1561{_}"Naturally I am not going to be such a fool as to advise that," Kelson returned.
10029{_}1562{_}"The discovery will be the subject of the servants' talk till it gets all over the place and into the papers.
10029{_}1563{_}No, what I have determined to do, unless you see any good reason for the contrary, is to go first thing in the morning to the police and tell them.
10029{_}1564{_}What do you say?"
10029{_}1565{_}he added sharply, as Gifford was silent.
10029{_}1566{_}"I should not do anything in a hurry," Gifford answered.
10029{_}1567{_}"But surely," Kelson remonstrated, "the sooner we take the line of putting ourselves in the right the better."
10029{_}1568{_}Again Gifford paused before replying.
10029{_}1569{_}"Can Miss Tredworth give no explanation, has she no idea as to how the stains came on her dress?"
10029{_}1570{_}"None whatever," was the emphatic answer.
10029{_}1571{_}"You are absolutely sure of that?"
10029{_}1572{_}Kelson jumped up from his chair.
10029{_}1573{_}"Hugh, what are you driving at?"
10029{_}1574{_}he cried, his eyes full of vague suspicion.
10029{_}1575{_}"I--I don't understand the cool way you are taking this.
10029{_}1576{_}There is something behind it.
10029{_}1577{_}Tell me.
10029{_}1578{_}I will know; I have a right."
10029{_}1579{_}Evidently the man was almost beside himself with the fear of something he could not comprehend.
10029{_}1580{_}Gifford rose and laid a hand sympathetically on his shoulder.
10029{_}1581{_}"I am sorry to seem so brutal, Harry," he said gently, "but this discovery does not surprise me."
10029{_}1582{_}Kelson recoiled as from a blow, staring at his friend with a horror-struck face.
10029{_}1583{_}"Why, good heavens, what do you mean?"
10029{_}1584{_}he gasped.
10029{_}1585{_}"Only," Gifford answered calmly, "that when you introduced me to Miss Tredworth at the dance I noticed the stains on the white flowers she wore."
10029{_}1586{_}"You did?"
10029{_}1587{_}Kelson was staring stupidly at Gifford.
10029{_}1588{_}"And you knew they were blood-stains?"
10029{_}1589{_}"I could not tell that," was the answer.
10029{_}1590{_}"But now it is pretty certain they were."
10029{_}1591{_}For some seconds neither man spoke.
10029{_}1592{_}Then with an effort Kelson seemed to nerve himself to put another question.
10029{_}1593{_}"Hugh," he said, his eyes pitiful with fear, "you--you don't think Muriel Tredworth had anything to do with Henshaw's death?"
10029{_}1594{_}Gifford turned away, and leaned on the mantelpiece.
10029{_}1595{_}"I don't know what to think," he said gloomily.
10029{_}1596{_}CHAPTER XI  GIFFORD'S COMMISSION   Next morning directly after breakfast Kelson started for Wynford Place.
10029{_}1597{_}As the result of deliberating fully upon the anxious problem before them, he and Gifford had come to the conclusion that it might be a grave mistake to try to keep secret the maid's discovery.
10029{_}1598{_}It would doubtless by this time have become a subject of gossip and speculation in the household and consequently would very soon become public.
10029{_}1599{_}Accordingly it was arranged that Kelson should arrive first and have a private interview with Muriel Tredworth with a view to ascertaining finally and for certain whether she could in any way account for the stain on her dress.
10029{_}1600{_}Gifford was to follow half an hour later, when they would have a conference with the Morristons and afterwards, with their approval, go into the town and see the chief constable on the subject.
10029{_}1601{_}If Gifford was doubtful as to the expediency of the plan, and it was with a considerable amount of hesitation that he brought himself to agree to it, he seemed to have no good reason to urge against it.
10029{_}1602{_}And, after all, it appeared, in the circumstances, the only politic course to follow.
10029{_}1603{_}Secrecy was practically now out of the question, and any attempt in that direction would inevitably fail and would in all probability produce results unpleasant to contemplate.
10029{_}1604{_}When Gifford arrived at Wynford Place he found Kelson pacing the drive and impatiently expecting him.
10029{_}1605{_}"Come along," he exclaimed, "the Morristons are waiting for us."
10029{_}1606{_}"Miss Tredworth--?"
10029{_}1607{_}"Is utterly unable to account for the state of her dress," Kelson declared promptly.
10029{_}1608{_}"She is positive that if she noticed the man she never spoke a word to him, nor danced with him.
10029{_}1609{_}She says that if she ever met him before, as according to that girl the other day was the case, she had quite forgotten the circumstance.
10029{_}1610{_}So the sooner we communicate this discovery to the police the better.
10029{_}1611{_}As it is, they say the servants are talking of it; so the present position is quite intolerable."
10029{_}1612{_}In the library they found Morriston and his sister with the Tredworths.
10029{_}1613{_}The situation was discussed and there seemed no doubt in the mind of any one of the party that the only thing to be done was to inform the police at once.
10029{_}1614{_}"The whole affair is so mysterious," Morriston said, "that all sorts of absurd rumours will be afloat if we don't take a strong, straightforward line at once.
10029{_}1615{_}Don't you agree, Edith?"
10029{_}1616{_}"Certainly I do," Miss Morriston answered with decision.
10029{_}1617{_}"I don't suppose," she added with a smile, "that any one would be mad enough to suggest, my dear Muriel, that you were in any way implicated in the affair; but the world is full of stupid and ill-natured people and one can't be too careful to put oneself in the right.
10029{_}1618{_}Don't you agree, Captain Kelson?"
10029{_}1619{_}"Most decidedly," Kelson replied, with a troubled face.
10029{_}1620{_}Charlie Tredworth was also quite emphatically of opinion that his sister should make no secret of what had been found.
10029{_}1621{_}"The inspector, who is here," Morriston said, "tells me that Major Freeman, our chief constable, intends to come here this morning.
10029{_}1622{_}I'll say we want to see him directly he arrives."
10029{_}1623{_}It was not long before the chief constable was shown into the library.
10029{_}1624{_}Morriston lost no time in telling him of the mysterious circumstance which had come to light.
10029{_}1625{_}Major Freeman, a keen soldierly man, with the stern expression and uncompromising manner naturally acquired by those whose business is to deal with crime, received the information with grave perplexity.
10029{_}1626{_}He turned a searching look upon Muriel Tredworth.
10029{_}1627{_}"I understand you are quite unable to account for the stains on your dress, Miss Tredworth?"
10029{_}1628{_}he asked in a tone of courteous insistence.
10029{_}1629{_}"Quite," she answered.
10029{_}1630{_}"I did not speak to Mr. Henshaw or even notice him in the ball-room."
10029{_}1631{_}"You had--pardon these questions; I am putting this in your own interest--you had at no time any acquaintance with Mr. Clement Henshaw?"
10029{_}1632{_}"I can hardly say that I had," the girl replied; "although a friend has told me that I played tennis with him at a garden-party some years ago."
10029{_}1633{_}"A circumstance which you do not recollect?"
10029{_}1634{_}The question was put politely, even sympathetically, yet with a certain uncomfortable directness.
10029{_}1635{_}"No," Muriel answered.
10029{_}1636{_}"Even when I was reminded of it, my recollection was of the vaguest description.
10029{_}1637{_}So far as that goes I could neither admit nor deny it with any certainty."
10029{_}1638{_}"And naturally you never, to your knowledge, saw or communicated with the deceased man since?"
10029{_}1639{_}Muriel flushed.
10029{_}1640{_}"No; absolutely no," she returned with a touch of resentment at the suggestion.
10029{_}1641{_}Major Freeman forbore to distress the girl by any further questioning.
10029{_}1642{_}"Thank you," he said simply.
10029{_}1643{_}"I am sorry to have even appeared to suggest such a thing, but you and your friends will appreciate that it was my duty to ask these questions.
10029{_}1644{_}This looks at the moment," he continued, addressing himself now to the party in general, "like proving a very mysterious, and I will add, peculiarly delicate affair.
10029{_}1645{_}The medical evidence is inclined to scout the idea of suicide, and my men who have the case in hand are coming round to the conclusion that the theory is untenable."
10029{_}1646{_}"The locked door--" Morriston suggested.
10029{_}1647{_}"The locked door," said Major Freeman, "presents a difficulty, but still one not absolutely incapable of solution.
10029{_}1648{_}We know," he added, with a faint smile, "from the way the door was eventually opened, that a key can be turned from the other side, given the right instrument to effect it."
10029{_}1649{_}"Which only a burglar or a locksmith would be likely to have," Kelson suggested.
10029{_}1650{_}Major Freeman nodded.
10029{_}1651{_}"Quite so.
10029{_}1652{_}I am not for a moment suggesting that as an explanation of the mystery.
10029{_}1653{_}It goes naturally much deeper than that.
10029{_}1654{_}Mr. Gervase Henshaw is to look into his brother's affairs and papers while in town, and I am hoping that on his return here he may be able to give some information which will afford a clue on which we can work.
10029{_}1655{_}In the meantime my men are not relaxing their efforts in this rather baffling case."
10029{_}1656{_}"In which," Morriston suggested, "this new piece of evidence does not afford any useful clue."
10029{_}1657{_}Major Freeman smiled, a little awkwardly, it seemed.
10029{_}1658{_}"If anything, it would appear to complicate the problem still further," he replied guardedly.
10029{_}1659{_}"Still, I am very glad to have it, and thank you for informing me so promptly.
10029{_}1660{_}Miss Tredworth may rest assured that should we find it necessary to go still farther into this piece of evidence, it will be done with as little annoyance as possible."
10029{_}1661{_}Some of the chief constable's habitual sternness of manner seemed to have returned to him as he now rose to take leave.
10029{_}1662{_}"I will just confer with my men who are on the premises before I leave," he said to Morriston in a quiet authoritative tone.
10029{_}1663{_}"They may have something to report."
10029{_}1664{_}With that he bowed to the company and quitted the room, leaving behind him a rather uncomfortable feeling which every one seemed to make an effort to throw off.
10029{_}1665{_}But there was clearly nothing to be done except to let the police researches take their course and to wait for developments.
10029{_}1666{_}The party at Wynford was going over to the dance at Stowgrave that evening and it was arranged that they would call for Kelson and Gifford and all go on together.
10029{_}1667{_}Accordingly at the appointed time the carriage stopped at the _Golden Lion_; Kelson joining Miss Tredworth and her brother, while Gifford drove with Morriston.
10029{_}1668{_}In answer to his companion's inquiry Morriston said that he had heard of nothing fresh in the Henshaw case.
10029{_}1669{_}"I saw Major Freeman for a moment as he was leaving," he said, "and gathered that the police were still at a loss for any satisfactory explanation as to how the crime was committed."
10029{_}1670{_}"He made no suggestion as to the stains on Miss Tredworth's dress?"
10029{_}1671{_}Gifford asked.
10029{_}1672{_}"No.
10029{_}1673{_}Although I fancy he is a good deal exercised by that piece of evidence.
10029{_}1674{_}Mentioned, as delicately as possible, that it might be necessary to have the stains analyzed, but did not wish the girl to be alarmed or worried about it.
10029{_}1675{_}I can't understand," Morriston added in a puzzled tone, "how on earth she could possibly have had anything to do with it."
10029{_}1676{_}"No," Gifford assented thoughtfully; "it is inconceivable, unless by the supposition that she may by some means have come in contact with some one who was concerned in the crime."
10029{_}1677{_}"You mean if a man had a stain on his coat and danced with her--"  "Something of the sort.
10029{_}1678{_}If there were blood on his lapel or sleeve."
10029{_}1679{_}"H'm!
10029{_}1680{_}It would be easy to ascertain for certain whom she danced with," Morriston said reflectively.
10029{_}1681{_}"But that again is almost unthinkable."
10029{_}1682{_}"And," Gifford added, "it seems to go no way towards elucidating the problem of how Henshaw came to his death.
10029{_}1683{_}As a matter of fact I should say Miss Tredworth danced and sat out nearly the whole of the evening with Kelson.
10029{_}1684{_}You know he proposed at the dance?"
10029{_}1685{_}"Yes, I understood that.
10029{_}1686{_}Poor Kelson; I am sorry for him, and for them both.
10029{_}1687{_}It is an ominous beginning of their betrothal."
10029{_}1688{_}"It is horrible," Gifford observed sympathetically.
10029{_}1689{_}"Although one tries to think there is really nothing in it for them to be concerned about."
10029{_}1690{_}The dance was an enjoyable affair, and, at any rate for the time, dispersed the depression which had hung over the party from Wynford.
10029{_}1691{_}Gifford had engaged Miss Morriston for two waltzes, and after a turn or two in the second his partner said she felt tired and suggested they should sit out the rest of it.
10029{_}1692{_}Accordingly they strolled off to an adjoining room and made themselves comfortable in a retired corner, Gifford, nothing loath to have a quiet chat with the handsome girl whose self-possessed manner with its suggestion of underlying strength of feeling was beginning to fascinate and intrigue his imagination.
10029{_}1693{_}"It is rather pleasant," she said a little wearily, "to get away from the atmosphere of mystery and police investigation we have been living in at home."
10029{_}1694{_}"Which I hope and believe will very soon be over," Gifford responded cheeringly.
10029{_}1695{_}Miss Morriston glanced at him curiously.
10029{_}1696{_}"You believe that?"
10029{_}1697{_}she returned almost sharply.
10029{_}1698{_}"How can you think so?
10029{_}1699{_}It seems to me that with little apparent likelihood of clearing up the mystery, the affair may drag on for weeks."
10029{_}1700{_}Gifford answered with a reassuring smile.
10029{_}1701{_}"Hardly that.
10029{_}1702{_}If the police can make nothing of it, and they seem to be quite nonplussed, they will have to give up their investigations and fall back on their first theory of suicide."
10029{_}1703{_}Leaning back and watching his companion's face in profile as she sat forward, he could see that his suggestion was by no means convincing.
10029{_}1704{_}"I wish I could take your view, Mr. Gifford," she returned, with the suggestion of a bitter smile.
10029{_}1705{_}"I dare say if the authorities were left to themselves they might give up.
10029{_}1706{_}But you forget a very potent factor in the tiresome business, the brother, Mr. Gervase Henshaw; he will keep them up to the work of investigation, will he not?"
10029{_}1707{_}"Up to a certain point, and one can scarcely blame him.
10029{_}1708{_}But even then, the police are not likely to continue working on his theories when they lead to no result."
10029{_}1709{_}"No?"
10029{_}1710{_}Miss Morriston replied in an unconvinced tone.
10029{_}1711{_}"But he is--" she turned to him.
10029{_}1712{_}"Tell me your candid opinion of this Mr. Gervase Henshaw.
10029{_}1713{_}Is he very--"  "Objectionable?"
10029{_}1714{_}Gifford supplied as she hesitated.
10029{_}1715{_}"Unpleasantly sharp and energetic, I should say.
10029{_}1716{_}Although it is, perhaps, hardly fair to judge a man labouring under the stress of a brother's tragic death."
10029{_}1717{_}"He is determined to get to the explanation of the mystery?"
10029{_}1718{_}The tinge of excitement she had exhibited in her former question had now passed away: she now spoke in her habitual cold, even tone.
10029{_}1719{_}"He says so.
10029{_}1720{_}Naturally he will do all he can to that end.
10029{_}1721{_}Of course it would be a satisfaction to know for certain how the tragedy came about: not that it matters much otherwise.
10029{_}1722{_}But unfortunately he rather poses as an expert in criminology, and that will make for pertinacity."
10029{_}1723{_}For a moment Miss Morriston kept silent.
10029{_}1724{_}"It is very unfortunate," she murmured at length.
10029{_}1725{_}"It will worry poor old Dick horribly.
10029{_}1726{_}I think he is already beginning to wish he had never seen Wynford."
10029{_}1727{_}Gifford leaned forward.
10029{_}1728{_}"Oh, but, my dear Miss Morriston," he said earnestly, "you and your brother must really not take the matter so seriously.
10029{_}1729{_}It is all very unpleasant, one must admit, but, after all, except that it happened in your house, I don't see that it affects you."
10029{_}1730{_}"You think not," Miss Morriston responded mechanically.
10029{_}1731{_}"Indeed I think so."
10029{_}1732{_}As he spoke Gifford could not help a slight feeling of wonder that this girl, from whom he would have expected an attitude rather of indifference, should allow herself to be so greatly worried by the affair.
10029{_}1733{_}For that she was far more troubled than she allowed to appear he was certain.
10029{_}1734{_}It is her pride, he told himself.
10029{_}1735{_}A high-bred girl like this would naturally hate the very idea of a sensational scandal under her roof, and all its unpleasant, rather sordid accompaniments.
10029{_}1736{_}"I wish," he added with a touch of fervour, "that I could persuade you to dismiss any fear of annoyance from your mind."
10029{_}1737{_}"I wish you could," she responded dully, with an attempt at a smile.
10029{_}1738{_}Suddenly she turned to him with more animation in her manner than she had hitherto shown.
10029{_}1739{_}"Mr. Gifford, you--I--" she hesitated as though at a loss how to put what she wished to say; "I have no right to ask you, who are a comparative stranger, to help us in this--this worry, but if you cared to be of assistance I am sure you could."
10029{_}1740{_}"Of course, of course I will," he answered with eager gladness.
10029{_}1741{_}"Only let me know what you wish and you may command the very utmost I can do.
10029{_}1742{_}And please don't think of me as a stranger."
10029{_}1743{_}Edith Morriston smiled, and to Gifford it was the most fascinating smile he had ever seen.
10029{_}1744{_}"Only let me know how I can serve you," he said, his pulses tingling.
10029{_}1745{_}"I am thinking of my brother," she replied, in a tone so friendly that it neutralized the rather damping effect of the words.
10029{_}1746{_}"He is worrying over this business more than one who does not know him well would think.
10029{_}1747{_}I had an idea, Mr. Gifford, that you might help us by, in a way, standing between us, so far as might be possible, and this Mr. Gervase Henshaw.
10029{_}1748{_}He stays at your hotel, does he not?"
10029{_}1749{_}"Yes; he is expected there to-morrow morning, if not to-night."
10029{_}1750{_}"You may perhaps," the girl proceeded, "be able--I don't know how, and I have no right to ask it--"  "Please, Miss Morriston!"
10029{_}1751{_}Gifford pleaded.
10029{_}1752{_}"To minimize any annoyance we are likely to suffer through his--his uncomfortable zeal," she resumed hesitatingly.
10029{_}1753{_}"If not that, you may, if he is friendly with you, have an opportunity of getting to hear something of his plans and ideas, and warning me if he is likely to worry us at Wynford.
10029{_}1754{_}We don't want the tragedy kept alive indefinitely; it would be intolerable.
10029{_}1755{_}I am sure you understand how I feel.
10029{_}1756{_}That is all."
10029{_}1757{_}"You may rely on me to the utmost," Gifford assured her fervently, in answer to the question in her eyes.
10029{_}1758{_}"Thank you," she said, as she rose.
10029{_}1759{_}"I felt sure I might ask you this favour and trust you."
10029{_}1760{_}She made a slight movement of putting out her hand.
10029{_}1761{_}The gesture was coldly made; it might, indeed, have been checked, and gone for nothing.
10029{_}1762{_}But Gifford, keenly on the alert for a sign of regard, was quick to take the hand and press it impulsively.
10029{_}1763{_}"You may trust me, Miss Morriston," he murmured.
10029{_}1764{_}"Thank you," she responded simply, but, he was glad to notice, with a touch of relief.
10029{_}1765{_}She lightly took his arm and they went back to the ball-room.
10029{_}1766{_}CHAPTER XII  HAD HENSHAW A CLUE?
10029{_}1767{_}Next day Gervase Henshaw made his expected reappearance in Branchester.
10029{_}1768{_}He left his luggage at the _Golden Lion_ and then went off to the police-station where he had a long interview with the chief constable.
10029{_}1769{_}Mindful of his promise to Edith Morriston, Hugh Gifford kept about the town with the object of coming across Henshaw and getting to know, if possible, something of his intentions.
10029{_}1770{_}The attraction he had, even from their first introduction, felt towards Miss Morriston had become quickly intensified by their strangely confidential talk on the previous evening.
10029{_}1771{_}So far she was to him something of a puzzle, but a puzzle of the most fascinating kind.
10029{_}1772{_}It was, perhaps, strangely unaccountable that she should have chosen to invoke his help who was little more than a casual acquaintance; still, he argued as he reviewed the situation, she had probably been drawn to him as the one man on the spot who was likely to be of use to them.
10029{_}1773{_}Her brother, a good, sensible fellow of some character, was nevertheless an ordinary country gentleman, given up to sport of all kinds and naturally quite unversed in the subtleties of life and character which can be studied only by those who live in the more intellectual atmosphere of cities.
10029{_}1774{_}The same judgment would apply to his friend Kelson, a chivalrous sportsman, who would unselfishly do anything in his power to be of help, but whose ability and penetration by no means matched his willingness.
10029{_}1775{_}And probably these men were types of the bulk of the Morristons' friends and acquaintances, at any rate of those who were immediately available.
10029{_}1776{_}Consequently, Gifford concluded, it had been to himself she had turned in this trouble, influenced no doubt by the idea that a Londoner with legal training and experience of the world in its many aspects would be the best man she could enlist to help her.
10029{_}1777{_}That her confidence had been drawn by any particular personal liking he never for one moment admitted; that unfortunately was so far all on one side, whatever hopes the future might hold out to him.
10029{_}1778{_}Anyhow he blessed his luck that an accident had so quickly broken the ice and established a state of confidential relationship between them.
10029{_}1779{_}As to there being an adequate reason for alarm Gifford was not inclined to question, since he quite realized that this man Henshaw might easily constitute himself a grave annoyance to the Morristons.
10029{_}1780{_}A clever girl like Edith Morriston, more sensitive than to a casual observer would appear, had naturally recognized this danger and was anxious to have the man, with his, perhaps, none too scrupulous methods, held in check; and to this service Gifford was only too happy to devote himself, glad beyond measure that the opportunity had been given him by the girl who had filled his thoughts.
10029{_}1781{_}It was not until evening that he came across Henshaw, it being to his mind essential not to appear anxious or to seek out the criminologist with the obvious view of getting information as to his plans.
10029{_}1782{_}"So you are back again, Mr. Henshaw," he said with a careless nod of greeting as they encountered in the hall of the hotel.
10029{_}1783{_}"I hear the police have not yet arrived at any satisfactory conclusion."
10029{_}1784{_}Henshaw drew back his lips in a slight smile.
10029{_}1785{_}To Gifford the expression was an ugly one, and he wondered what it portended.
10029{_}1786{_}"There is a likelihood of our not being at a loss much longer," Henshaw replied, speaking through his teeth with a certain grim satisfaction.
10029{_}1787{_}"What, you have made a discovery?"
10029{_}1788{_}Gifford exclaimed.
10029{_}1789{_}Henshaw's face hardened.
10029{_}1790{_}"I am not yet at liberty to say what I have found," he returned in an uncompromising tone.
10029{_}1791{_}"But I think you may take it from me as absolutely certain that my brother did not take his own life."
10029{_}1792{_}With pursed lips Gifford nodded acceptance of the statement.
10029{_}1793{_}"That makes the affair look serious, not to say sensational," he responded.
10029{_}1794{_}"I suppose one must not ask you whether you have a clue to the perpetrator."
10029{_}1795{_}"No, I can hardly say that yet," Henshaw answered with a rather cunning look.
10029{_}1796{_}"You, as one of our profession, Mr. Gifford, will understand that and the unwisdom of premature statements."
10029{_}1797{_}"Certainly I do," Gifford agreed promptly.
10029{_}1798{_}"And am quite content to restrain my curiosity till I get information from the papers."
10029{_}1799{_}Henshaw laughed intriguingly.
10029{_}1800{_}"There are certain things that don't find their way into the Press," he said meaningly.
10029{_}1801{_}"The real story in this case may turn out to be one of them."
10029{_}1802{_}Eager as he was, Gifford resolved to show no further curiosity.
10029{_}1803{_}"You know best," he rejoined almost casually.
10029{_}1804{_}"But I hope for the Morristons' sake the mystery will be soon satisfactorily cleared up."
10029{_}1805{_}There was a peculiar glitter in Henshaw's eyes as he replied, "No doubt they are anxious."
10029{_}1806{_}"Naturally.
10029{_}1807{_}They are getting rather worried by all this police fuss."
10029{_}1808{_}"Naturally."
10029{_}1809{_}Henshaw repeated Gifford's word with a curious emphasis.
10029{_}1810{_}"It is unfortunate for them," he added.
10029{_}1811{_}"But all the same it is imperative that the manner of my brother's death should be thoroughly investigated."
10029{_}1812{_}He nodded, and as unwilling to discuss the matter further, opened a newspaper and turned away.
10029{_}1813{_}About noon next day Gifford went with Kelson to Wynford Place.
10029{_}1814{_}They had seen nothing more of Henshaw who, it seemed, was rather inclined to hold away from them, possibly with a view to avoiding an opportunity of discussing the affair, or because he was occupied in following up some clue he had, or thought he had, got hold of.
10029{_}1815{_}This was naturally a disappointment to Gifford, who was anxious, on Miss Morriston's behalf, to keep himself posted as to Henshaw's intentions.
10029{_}1816{_}"Of course," said Kelson, "the fellow will have heard of the stains found on Muriel's dress, and will set himself to make the most of that discovery.
10029{_}1817{_}I only hope he won't take to worrying her.
10029{_}1818{_}She is quite enough upset about it without that."
10029{_}1819{_}"Doubtless that is why he is keeping away from us," Gifford observed.
10029{_}1820{_}"He probably has heard of your engagement."
10029{_}1821{_}"And has the decency to see that he cannot very well discuss the matter with us," Kelson added.
10029{_}1822{_}On their arrival at Wynford Place Morriston told them that Gervase Henshaw was there with a detective in the room of the tragedy.
10029{_}1823{_}"There is a decided improvement in his manner to-day," he said with a laugh.
10029{_}1824{_}"He has been quite considerate and apologetic; so much so that I think I shall have to ask him to stay to luncheon; it seems rather churlish in the circumstances not to do so when the man is actually in the house on what should be to him a very sad business.
10029{_}1825{_}But you fellows must stay too, to take off some of the strain."
10029{_}1826{_}They accepted; Gifford not sorry, for more reasons than one, to stay.
10029{_}1827{_}He presently took an opportunity of joining Edith Morriston in the garden.
10029{_}1828{_}"I have been keeping a look-out for Mr. Henshaw," he said, as they strolled off down a secluded walk, "but so far have had a chance of speaking to him only once, when I ran across him in the hotel."
10029{_}1829{_}"Yes?"
10029{_}1830{_}she responded, with a scarcely concealed curiosity to hear what had passed.
10029{_}1831{_}"He has evidently got hold of some clue, or at least thinks he has," Gifford proceeded.
10029{_}1832{_}"But what it is he did not tell me.
10029{_}1833{_}In fact he rather declined to discuss the affair.
10029{_}1834{_}I fancy he had had a long consultation with the police authorities."
10029{_}1835{_}"And he would tell you nothing?"
10029{_}1836{_}"Nothing.
10029{_}1837{_}I rather expected he might have come, as before, to discuss the case with us, but he has made a point of keeping away.
10029{_}1838{_}I hear, however, from your brother that he seems far less objectionable this time."
10029{_}1839{_}Somewhat to Gifford's surprise, she gave a rather grudging assent.
10029{_}1840{_}"Yes, I suppose he is.
10029{_}1841{_}I happened to see him on his arrival, and he certainly was polite enough, but it is possible to be even objectionably polite."
10029{_}1842{_}Gifford glanced at her curiously, wondering what had taken place to call forth the remark.
10029{_}1843{_}"I know that," he said.
10029{_}1844{_}"I do hope the man has not annoyed you.
10029{_}1845{_}From what your brother told us--"  "Oh, no," she interrupted, "I can't say he has annoyed me--from his point of view."
10029{_}1846{_}She laughed.
10029{_}1847{_}"The man tried to be particularly agreeable, I think."
10029{_}1848{_}"And succeeded in being the reverse," Gifford added.
10029{_}1849{_}"I can quite understand.
10029{_}1850{_}Still, it might be worse."
10029{_}1851{_}"Oh, yes," she agreed in a tone which did nothing to abate his curiosity.
10029{_}1852{_}The luncheon bell rang out and they turned.
10029{_}1853{_}"I haven't thanked you for looking after our interests, Mr. Gifford," the girl said.
10029{_}1854{_}"I have unfortunately been able to do nothing," he replied deprecatingly.
10029{_}1855{_}"But you have tried," she rejoined graciously, "and it is not your fault if you have not succeeded.
10029{_}1856{_}It is a comfort to think that we have a friend at hand ready to help us if need be, and I am most grateful."
10029{_}1857{_}The unusual feeling in her tone thrilled him.
10029{_}1858{_}"I should love to do something worthy of your gratitude," he responded, in a subdued tone.
10029{_}1859{_}"You take a lower view of your service than I do," she rejoined as they reached the house, and no more could be said.
10029{_}1860{_}At luncheon the improvement which their host had mentioned in Henshaw's attitude was strikingly apparent.
10029{_}1861{_}His dogmatic self-assertiveness which had before been found so irritating was laid aside; his manner was subdued, his tone was sympathetic as he apologized for all the annoyance to which his host and hostess were being put.
10029{_}1862{_}Gifford, watching him alertly, wondered at the change, and more particularly at its cause, which set him speculating.
10029{_}1863{_}What did it portend?
10029{_}1864{_}It seemed as though the complete alteration in the man's attitude and manner might indicate that he had got the solution of the mystery, and no longer had that problem to worry him.
10029{_}1865{_}Certainly there was little to find fault with in him to-day.
10029{_}1866{_}One thing, however, Gifford did not like, and that was Henshaw's rather obvious admiration for Edith Morriston.
10029{_}1867{_}When they took their places at table, she had motioned to Gifford to sit beside her, and from that position it gradually forced itself upon his notice that Henshaw scarcely took his eyes off his hostess, addressing most of his conversation, and he was a fluent talker, to her.
10029{_}1868{_}It was, of course, scarcely to be wondered at that this handsome, capable girl should call forth any man's admiration.
10029{_}1869{_}Gifford himself was indeed beginning to fall desperately in love with her, but this naturally made Henshaw's rather obvious prepossession none the less disagreeable to him.
10029{_}1870{_}This, then, he reflected, was the explanation of what Miss Morriston had hinted at, what she had described as his objectionable excess of politeness at their meeting that morning.
10029{_}1871{_}Happily, however, Gifford felt secure in his position as her accredited ally and in her expressed dislike to the man whom it seemed she had unwittingly fascinated.
10029{_}1872{_}It was indeed unthinkable that this splendid, high-bred girl could ever be responsive to the advances of this unpleasantly sharp, rather underbred man, and he was a little surprised that she could respond to his remarks quite so genially, with more graciousness indeed than even her position as hostess called forth.
10029{_}1873{_}He could not quite reconcile it with the way she had spoken of him previously; but then he told himself that he was making too much of the business, and saw what was mere politeness through the magnifying glasses of jealousy.
10029{_}1874{_}And so, secure in his position, he proceeded to view Henshaw's attempts to ingratiate himself with an amused equanimity.
10029{_}1875{_}CHAPTER XIII  WHAT GIFFORD SAW IN THE WOOD   During the next day or two Gifford saw next to nothing of Gervase Henshaw.
10029{_}1876{_}They had parted amicably enough after luncheon at Wynford Place; indeed, the change in Henshaw's demeanour had been something of a puzzle to the two friends, although Kelson did not seem much exercised by it.
10029{_}1877{_}"The fellow has evidently come to the conclusion that in dealing with people like the Morristons an offensive brow-beating manner does not pay," he remarked casually.
10029{_}1878{_}Gifford, however, had an idea that the reason for the change lay somewhat deeper than that.
10029{_}1879{_}He wondered whether in the absence of any other apparent cause, Edith Morriston's attractiveness had had anything to do with it.
10029{_}1880{_}It was not a pleasant idea; still, if it saved her annoyance that would be something gained, he thought; and that it should have any farther result was out of the question.
10029{_}1881{_}He had not had that day an opportunity of any private talk with Miss Morriston, for she had driven out after luncheon to pay a call.
10029{_}1882{_}But a certain suggestion of warmth in her leave-taking had assured him that she still looked for his help and that the conditions were not changed.
10029{_}1883{_}What he had undertaken so eagerly was now, however, not easy of accomplishment.
10029{_}1884{_}For reasons at which Gifford could only guess, Henshaw seemed to be playing an elusive game; he kept out of sight, or, at any rate, avoided all intercourse with the two friends, and on the rare occasions when they met he was to Gifford tantalizingly uncommunicative.
10029{_}1885{_}That something was evidently behind his reticence made it all the more unsatisfactory, since the result was that Gifford had no object in going to Wynford Place, for he had nothing to tell.
10029{_}1886{_}Indeed he learnt more from the Morristons than from Henshaw.
10029{_}1887{_}The police had concluded their investigations on the premises, much to the relief of the household, who were now left in peace.
10029{_}1888{_}"They don't seem to have come to any definite conclusion as to how the tragedy happened," Morriston said.
10029{_}1889{_}"They have an idea, as I gather from Major Freeman, where to look for the murderer, if murder it was; which I am rather inclined to doubt."
10029{_}1890{_}"Is Henshaw likely to give up the search?"
10029{_}1891{_}Gifford asked.
10029{_}1892{_}Morriston looked puzzled.
10029{_}1893{_}"I can't make out," he answered in a slightly perplexed tone.
10029{_}1894{_}"Even Freeman does not seem to know what his idea is.
10029{_}1895{_}He is still about here."
10029{_}1896{_}"Yes," Gifford replied.
10029{_}1897{_}"I caught a glimpse of him this morning."
10029{_}1898{_}"Curious," Morriston remarked.
10029{_}1899{_}"I came across the fellow yesterday afternoon in the big plantation here.
10029{_}1900{_}He was mooning about and didn't seem best pleased to see me, but he was quite duly apologetic, said he was puzzling over the tragedy and hoped I didn't mind his trespassing on my property.
10029{_}1901{_}Of course I told him he was free to come and go as he liked, but it did strike me as peculiar that he should be thinking out the case in that plantation which has no possible connexion with the scene of the crime."
10029{_}1902{_}"Yes, it was curious," Gifford agreed reflectively.
10029{_}1903{_}"Did he tell you what he was doing about the business?"
10029{_}1904{_}Morriston shook his head.
10029{_}1905{_}"No; he wasn't communicative; didn't seem to have much to go upon.
10029{_}1906{_}Of course one can't tell what the fellow has at the back of his mind, but I was rather surprised that a Londoner of his energy and smartness should spend his time loafing about down here with what seems a poor chance of any result; and I nearly told him so."
10029{_}1907{_}"Perhaps it is as well you didn't," Gifford replied.
10029{_}1908{_}"He is suspicious enough to imagine you might have a motive in wanting to get rid of him."
10029{_}1909{_}Morriston laughed.
10029{_}1910{_}"I have.
10029{_}1911{_}He is not exactly the man one wants to have prowling about the place; but it would not be polite to hint as much."
10029{_}1912{_}The episode, trivial as it seemed to Morriston, gave Gifford food for disagreeable reflection.
10029{_}1913{_}Why, indeed, should Henshaw be hanging about in the grounds of Wynford, and give so unconvincing a reason?
10029{_}1914{_}What troubled Gifford most was that the man's reticent attitude precluded all hope of his learning anything of his plans which could usefully be imparted to Miss Morriston.
10029{_}1915{_}Evidently there was nothing to be got out of him; the rather open confidence he had displayed on his first appearance at Branchester had quite disappeared, and if Gifford was to find out anything worth reporting it would assuredly not be due to any communication from the man himself.
10029{_}1916{_}He had accordingly to be content with the resolve to keep a wary eye on Henshaw's movements.
10029{_}1917{_}He was now pretty free to do this.
10029{_}1918{_}The Tredworths had ended their visit at Wynford and had returned home, and naturally Kelson spent much of his time over there, leaving Gifford to his own devices.
10029{_}1919{_}It had, in view of Gifford's commission from Miss Morriston, been arranged that he should share Kelson's rooms at the _Golden Lion_, no longer as a guest, so that both men were now independent of each other.
10029{_}1920{_}The date of Kelson's wedding seemed now likely to be put off for some months, as his friend had suggested.
10029{_}1921{_}The unpleasant episode of the stains on Muriel Tredworth's dress had, although there was no indication of attaching serious importance to them, nevertheless cast an uncomfortable shadow over the happiness of her betrothal, and without giving any specific reason she had declared for a postponement of the wedding, for which there was, after all, a quite natural reason.
10029{_}1922{_}"Perhaps it is just as well," Kelson remarked to his friend.
10029{_}1923{_}"Although it is absolutely unthinkable that Muriel could have had anything to do with the affair, yet one can quite appreciate her wish to wait till perhaps something crops up to give us the explanation beyond all question.
10029{_}1924{_}It is rather a blow to me, and I hope if the mysterious Mr. Gervase Henshaw is really on the track of the crime he will produce his solution without much more delay.
10029{_}1925{_}For a girl like Muriel to have even the faintest suspicion hanging over her is simply hateful."
10029{_}1926{_}Meanwhile the mysterious Mr. Henshaw seemed in no hurry to make known his theory, if he had one.
10029{_}1927{_}Yet he still remained in Branchester, writing all the morning and going out in the afternoon, usually with a handful of letters for post.
10029{_}1928{_}He always nodded affably to Gifford when they met, but beyond a casual remark on the weather or the events of the day, showed no disposition to chat.
10029{_}1929{_}But now while Gifford was in this unsatisfactory state of mind, persevering yet baffled in what he had undertaken to do, a very singular thing came to pass.
10029{_}1930{_}He strolled out one afternoon, aimlessly, wondering whether the negative result of his efforts justified his remaining in the place, and yet loath to leave it, held there as he was by the attraction of Edith Morriston.
10029{_}1931{_}He felt he could be making but little way in her favour seeing how he was failing in what he had undertaken to do for her, and as he walked he discussed with himself whether it would not be possible to hit on some more active plan of becoming acquainted with Henshaw's knowledge and intentions.
10029{_}1932{_}It was obviously a delicate business, and after all, he thought, now that the man's undesirable presence had practically ceased to be an annoyance to the Morristons there scarcely seemed any need to bother about him.
10029{_}1933{_}On the other hand, however, there was a certain strong curiosity on his own part to know Henshaw's design and what kept him in the town.
10029{_}1934{_}Gifford's walk took him over well remembered ground.
10029{_}1935{_}He was strolling along a path which led through the Wynford property, over a rustic bridge across a stream he had often fished when a boy, and so on into a wood which formed one of the home coverts.
10029{_}1936{_}Making his way through this familiar haunt of by-gone days he came to one of the long rides which bisected the wood for some quarter of a mile.
10029{_}1937{_}He turned into this and was just looking out for a comfortable trunk where he might sit and smoke, when he caught sight of two figures in the distance ahead walking slowly just on the fringe of the ride.
10029{_}1938{_}A man and a woman; their backs were towards him, but his blood gave a leap at the sight as their identity flashed upon him.
10029{_}1939{_}It was, in its unexpectedness, an almost appalling sight to him, as he realised that the two were none other than Henshaw and Edith Morriston.
10029{_}1940{_}CHAPTER XIV  GIFFORD'S PERPLEXITY   Next moment Gifford had instinctively sprung back into the covert of the trees, almost dazed by what he had seen.
10029{_}1941{_}Henshaw and Edith Morriston!
10029{_}1942{_}Could it be possible?
10029{_}1943{_}His eyes must have deceived him.
10029{_}1944{_}About the girl there could be no doubt.
10029{_}1945{_}Her tall, graceful figure was unmistakable.
10029{_}1946{_}But the man.
10029{_}1947{_}Surely he had been mistaken there; it must have been her brother, or perhaps a friend who had been lunching with them.
10029{_}1948{_}Had Gifford, his mind obsessed by Henshaw, jumped to a false conclusion?
10029{_}1949{_}He stooped, and creeping warily beyond the fringe of trees looked after the pair.
10029{_}1950{_}They were now some thirty yards away.
10029{_}1951{_}There could be no doubt that the lady was Edith Morriston; and the man?
10029{_}1952{_}Incredible as it might seem, he was surely Gervase Henshaw.
10029{_}1953{_}Gifford had seen him some two hours earlier, and now recognized his grey suit and dark felt hat.
10029{_}1954{_}He stayed, crouched down, looking after the amazing pair, seeking a sign that the man was not Henshaw.
10029{_}1955{_}After all, it was, he told himself, more likely that he had made a mistake than that Miss Morriston could be strolling in confidential talk (for such seemed the case) with that fellow.
10029{_}1956{_}It was too astounding for belief.
10029{_}1957{_}They had stopped now, at the end of the ride; the man talking earnestly, it seemed; Miss Morriston standing with head bent down and scoring the grass with her walking-stick as though in doubt or consideration.
10029{_}1958{_}Would they turn and put the man's identity beyond uncertainty?
10029{_}1959{_}Gifford had not long to wait.
10029{_}1960{_}Miss Morriston seemed to draw off and began to walk back down the ride; her companion turned and promptly put himself by her side.
10029{_}1961{_}There was no doubt now as to who he was.
10029{_}1962{_}Gervase Henshaw.
10029{_}1963{_}As one glance, now that the face was revealed, proved that, Gifford drew back quickly and hurried deeper into the thick wood fearful lest his footsteps should be heard.
10029{_}1964{_}When he had gone a safe distance an intense curiosity made him halt and turn.
10029{_}1965{_}From his place of hiding he could just see the light of the ride along which the couple would pass.
10029{_}1966{_}He hated the idea of spying upon Edith Morriston; after all, if she chose to walk and talk with this man it was no business of his; but a supreme distrust of Henshaw, unreasonable enough, perhaps, but none the less keen, made him suspicious that the man might be playing some cowardly game, might have drawn the girl to him by unfair means.
10029{_}1967{_}Otherwise it was surely inconceivable that she should have consented--condescended indeed--to meet him in that clandestine manner.
10029{_}1968{_}As Gifford stayed, hesitating between a breach of good form and a legitimate desire to learn whether the girl was being subjected to unfair treatment, the sound of Henshaw's rather penetrating voice came into earshot, and a few seconds later they passed across the line of Gifford's sight.
10029{_}1969{_}He could catch but a glimpse of them through the intervening trees as they went by slowly, but it was enough to tell him that Henshaw was talking earnestly, arguing, it seemed, and on Edith Morriston's clear-cut face was a look of trouble which was not good to see.
10029{_}1970{_}It made Gifford flush with anger to think that this lovely high-bred girl was being worried, probably being made love to, by a man of that objectionable type; for that she could be in that situation without coercion was not to be believed.
10029{_}1971{_}The reason for Henshaw's prolonged and rather puzzling stay in the place was now accounted for.
10029{_}1972{_}Moreover, to Gifford's bitter reflection the whole business seemed clear enough.
